Jamison Brereton

76 (592)
Dawn
Vasi·π£·π≠ha MaitrƒÅvaru·πái
7 verses: tri·π£·π≠ubh
A tightly structured hymn: the verbally responsive verses 3‚Äì4 are set within an inner ring (vss. 2, 5) with exact repetition in their final pƒÅdas (√°mardhanto v√°subhi·∏•…). This

structure is reinforced by tense usage: the verbs in verses 1‚Äì2 are all augmented aor ists referring to the immediate past of today‚Äôs dawn; those in verses 5‚Äì7 are present indicatives and imperatives, stating general truths about the behavior of the dawns and the singers; while those in verses 3‚Äì4 are augmented imperfects (with one per
fect) referring to the distant past, which gave rise to the present situation. This structure defines verses 3‚Äì4 as an omphalos, and indeed these verses show clear omphalos features. Verse 3 contains the most intricate syntax in the hymn, while verse 4 concerns the Father‚Äôs finding of ‚Äúthe hidden light‚Äù in the Vala cave, the primeval model for each new dawn. Verses 3 and 4 are in reverse chronologi cal order: the Fathers find the hidden light of Dawn in verse 4, and verse 3 then describes the subsequent time with the periodic return of the Dawn, eagerly pro ceeding to the sacrifice as if to a lover, not moving slowly and reluctantly like a maiden leaving an assignation.
The exact repetition in the inner ring mentioned above is not a static feature. It participates in what we have termed ‚Äúpoetic repair‚Äù (Jamison 2006) and therefore provides forward movement by setting up a puzzle that is resolved later in the hymn. The adjective √°mardhanta·∏• ‚Äúnot negligent‚Äù modifies ‚Äúpaths‚Äù in verse 2. The com
bination seems discordant, although the hearer can construct a meaningful read ing: paths that are not negligent are those, properly maintained and ‚Äúset in order,‚Äù that lead one where they are supposed to go. The application of ‚Äúnot negligent‚Äù to the priests in verse 5 is far easier to interpret: not negligent priests are those who properly perform the sacrifice every morning and therefore do not transgress the gods‚Äô commandments that regulate the proper functioning of the cosmos, since by ritual logic the sacrifice causes the new day to dawn. The phrase in verse 5 thus ‚Äúrepairs‚Äù the more jarring one in verse 2, but the jarring phrase in verse 2 also forced the hearers to formulate a more complex conception.
As so often, consideration of poetic structure gives depth to the apparently conventional expressions and images in this hymn, which recur in so many other descriptions of Dawn.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Dawn On the intricate structure of this hymn and its relationship to verb tense, see published introduction. As noted there, vss. 1-2 have augmented aorists referring to the immediate past (√°≈õret 1b, ajani·π£·π≠a 1c, akar 1d, ad·πõ≈õran 2a, √°bh≈´t 2c, ƒÅÃÅgƒÅt 2d); vss. 3- 4 have augmented imperfects and one perfect referring to the more distant past (ƒÅsan 3a, 4a, dad·πõk·π£√© 3d, avindan 4c, ajanayan 4d); and vss. 5-7 have present indicatives and imperatives stating general truths and urging action (s√°·πÉ jƒÅnate ‚Ä¶ yatante 5b, minanti 5c, ƒ´·∏∑ate 6a, ucha 6c, jarasva 6d, ribhyate 7b).

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúThe divine SavitƒÅ, the leader of all, sends upwards the immortal, all- benefiting light; the eye of thegods has been manifested for (the celebration of) religious rites; the Dawn has made all creatures visible.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

The immortal light belonging to all people‚Äîhe who belongs to all men, god Savitar, has propped that up.
In accordance with his will, the eye of the gods has been born. Dawn has revealed the whole world.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Unlike the previous hymn, which contains no other divinities, this vs. introduces two (though one without name) before mentioning U·π£as, who enters only as the very last word of the vs. The two other gods are Savitar (b) and S≈´rya in his role as ‚Äúeye of the gods‚Äù (devƒÅÃÅnƒÅm ‚Ä¶ c√°k·π£u·∏•, c).

The two virtually synonymous adj. vi≈õv√°janya- ‚Äòbelonging to all people‚Äô and vi≈õvƒÅÃÅnara- ‚Äòbelonging to all men‚Äô are juxtaposed across the pƒÅda boundary (a/b); they refer to two different entities: the immortal light (jy√≥tir am·πõÃ•ÃÅtam), presumably the sun, and god Savitar (savitƒÅÃÅdev√°·∏•). As such they may also subtly allude to the wellknown group, the All Gods, with their first member(s) vi≈õv√°- and the ‚Äòmen‚Äô words implicitly summoning up the opposite, dev√°-. The pl. gods then show up in c, with another occurrence of v√≠≈õva- in d.

In c it is not possible to determine whose kr√°tu- is being referred to. Geldner takes it as the gods‚Äô, and certainly the adjacency of the two words (kr√°tvƒÅ devƒÅÃÅnƒÅm) is suggestive. Renou seems to favor U·π£as. However, given that it is Savitar‚Äôs action in ab that raised the light, I think it likely that the kr√°tu- is his.

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§¨ ‡§â‡§∑‡§æ=‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§π‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡§•‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç‡•§

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡§ø‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ (‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§Ç) ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§ (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§° ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§∞‡§É) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§° ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ï (‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ) ‡§∏‡§¨‡§ï‡§æ ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§® (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§É) ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó (‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç, ‡§ú‡•ã (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ) ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§ø‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ï‡•á (‡§Ö‡§ú‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§´‡§≤‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, (‡§≠‡•Å‡§µ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§≠‡•Å‡§µ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ (‡§â‡§∑‡§æ‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§ï (‡§â‡§§‡•ç) ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§Ü‡§µ‡§ø‡§É ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§É) ‡§ö‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡•Ä (‡§Ö‡§ï‡§É) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç‡••‡•ß‡••

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§ó‡§à ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ‡§∏‡§¨ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ì‡§§‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã ‡§∞‡§π‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§∏‡§¨‡§ï‡§æ ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§® ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§ø‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§â‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§∏‡§¨‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡§æ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§è ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§â‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§π‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§≤‡§ó‡•ç‡§® ‡§π‡•ã‡§®‡§æ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§è, ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§ö‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§π‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•É‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø ‡§π‡•à ‡•• ‡§ï‡§à ‡§è‡§ï ‡§ü‡•Ä‡§ï‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§®‡•á ‡§Ø‡§π‡§æ‡§Å ‚Äú‡§â‡§∑‡§æ‚Äù ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§â‡§®‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§≤ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ø ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡§ø, ‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•É‡§§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡§ï ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§ï‡§ø‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§ú‡•ú ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§ï‡•á ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§∏‡§∞‡•Ä ‡§¨‡§æ‡§§ ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§â‡§∑‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ó‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§™‡•Ä‡§õ‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡§æ ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡•á‡§Ø ‡§π‡•à, ‡§á‡§∏‡§≤‡§ø‡§è ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ã‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ï‡§ø‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§ú‡•ú ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡••‡•ß‡••

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø (‡§∏‡§Ç) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∑‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡•á (‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§π‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•á) ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡•§

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø (‡§∏‡§Ç)- ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡§ø‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç (‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§ñ‡§ø‡§≤‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§Ç (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§∞‡§É) ‡§∏‡§ï‡§≤‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ï‡§Ç (‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§É) ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø, ‡§Ø‡§É (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡§É (‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ) ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§Ö‡§ú‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§´‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø (‡§≠‡•Å‡§µ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∏‡§ï‡§≤‡§≠‡•Å‡§µ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç (‡§â‡§∑‡§æ‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§ï‡§É (‡§â‡§§‡•ç) ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§ö (‡§Ü‡§µ‡§ø‡§É ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§É) ‡§ö‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç, ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•å ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø, ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç (‡§Ö‡§ï‡§É) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ ‡••‡•ß‡••

Jamison Brereton

The paths leading to the gods have become visible to me‚Äîpaths that are not negligent and are set in order by the good ones.
The beacon of Dawn has appeared from the east. She has come here, facing west from her habitation.

Jamison Brereton Notes

On the relation of this vs. to its paired frame vs. 5, see published introduction. Their relationship is signalled in the first instance by patterned repetition, with 2b and 5b almost identical: √°mardhanto v√°subhi·∏• x x x x. This patterned repetition also involves poetic repair. The qualifier √°mardhanta·∏• ‚Äònot negligent‚Äô, used unusually of paths in vs. 2b, returns in 5d with a far more appropriate referent, the Fathers or their modern-day representatives, the Vasi·π£·π≠has. The standard tr. either ignore the identity of the two words, found in the same metrical position, and tr. each in a way that fits the context as the tr. sees it (so Geldner ‚Äúunfehlbar‚Äù 2b versus ‚Äúnicht zur√ºckstehend‚Äù 2d) or choose an anodyne tr. that doesn‚Äôt reflect the act. transitive morphology of the form (Renou ‚Äúimpeccable‚Äù in both places). But forms of the root ‚àöm·πõdh generally take an acc. obj. (or an enclitic prn. that is likely acc.) in the sense ‚Äòneglect X‚Äô, and we would expect the participle, even negated, to reflect the same usage. As usual, I think it is incumbent on us to follow the morphology, even when it leads us to interpretations that seem, at first, awkward. Here I would first point out that Dawn 100 ‚Äúawakened‚Äù the paths in the previous hymn (VII.75.1d pathyƒÅÃÄajƒ´ga·∏•), so paths in this group of hymns appear to have more animacy than might be expected. The paths in our vs. are the ones that lead to the gods (devayƒÅÃÅnƒÅ·∏•), and in this context ‚Äúnonnegligent paths‚Äù could be ones that don‚Äôt fail to lead us there, perhaps because they stay in good order, as is implied by the qualifier √≠·π£k·πõta-. As often with such semantic mismatches, the sense that comes from apparently incompatible words construed together is hard won, but it also leads to a deeper understanding of what the poet intended.

In the ppl. √≠·π£k·πõta- here and in a number of other locutions involving √≠·π£ + ‚àök·πõ (i·π£kart√°r-, √≠·π£k·πõti-, etc.), √≠·π£- behaves like a pseudo-preverb. The most likely default source for this √≠·π£- is the root noun of the same shape meaning ‚Äòrefreshment, nourishing drink‚Äô (so EWA s.v. √≠·π£-), although the semantics makes difficulties: the additive meaning we might expect (‚Äòprepare nourishment‚Äô vel sim.) is not found.

Instead it seems to mean something like ‚Äòset in order, set to rights, restore‚Äô. Although some interpr. the idiom as ‚Äòheal‚Äô (see EWA loc. cit.), I see no good evidence for this in the RV; certainly ‚Äúhealed paths‚Äù here would be even more aberrant than ‚Äúnonnegligent‚Äù ones. The form here is the only occurrence of the lexeme √≠·π£ ‚àök·πõ in the Family Books; otherwise it is limited to the late RV: the finite verb √≠·π£k·πõ·πáudhvam X.53.7, the past participle here and in the cmpd. √≠·π£k·πõtƒÅhƒÅva- X.101.6, as well as negated √°ni·π£k·πõta- VIII.99.8 and IX.39.2, agent nouns √≠·π£kartar- VIII.1.12 and i·π£kart√°r- VIII.99.8, X.140.5, and the fem. abstract √≠·π£k·πõti- X.97.9. Besides its possible etymological connection with √≠·π£- ‚Äòrefreshment‚Äô (textually hinted at only in IX.39.2), it also seems to form an antonymic pair with n√≠·π£ ‚àök·πõ ‚Äòexpel‚Äô; see the hymn to healing herbs, X.97, where √≠·π£k·πõti- is contrasted with n√≠·π£k·πõti-, n√≠·π£ k·πõtha. This rhyming contrast may account for the ‚Äòrestore‚Äô sense, antonymic to ‚Äòexpel‚Äô. Our passage also contains interaction with a different pseudo-preverb: √≠·π£-k·πõta- can be seen as picking up (ƒÅv)√≠r akar in 1c. Though the augment induces -r sandhi, the underlying idiom is ƒÅv√≠·π£ ‚àök·πõ (e.g., IV.4.5 ƒÅv√≠·π£ k·πõ·πáu·π£va) with -√≠·π£ matching √≠·π£k·πõta- here.

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§ó‡§à ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§ú‡§¨ ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§ï ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¶‡•á‡§ñ ‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ö‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§§ ‡§∏‡§¨‡§∏‡•á ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•ã‡§Æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§§‡§¨ ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ï‡§£‡•ç‡§† ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§ï‡§π‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ù‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã, ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§π‡•ã ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡§ø, ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§∏‡§¨ ‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§¶‡•Ä‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§π‡•ã ‡§∞‡§π‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à, ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§ï ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§á‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ ‡§ï‡•ã ‚Äú‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡•Ä ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ó‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡§ß‡§ø‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡•¶‚Äù‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ-‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∂‡§æ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡§ø‡§∂‡§æ‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§ß‡§ø‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§è‡§ï ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§π‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ï, ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§ï ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§¶‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§â‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•Ä ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§π‡•à, ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡••‡•®‡••

  • yatƒ´va ‚Üê yatƒ´ ‚Üê i
  • [verb noun], nominative, singular
  • ‚Äúgo; travel; enter (a state); return; walk; continue; reach; ask.‚Äù
  • yatƒ´va ‚Üê iva
  • [adverb]
  • ‚Äúlike; as it were; somehow; just so.‚Äù

‡§π‡•á ‡§â‡§∑‡§É ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ ‡§§‡§µ ‡§§‡•á‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§ø ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§Ö‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§Ü‡§∏‡§®‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§â‡§∑‡§É‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§µ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§π‡§É‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§π‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§â‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§â‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡•Å‡§¶‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∏‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡•Ä‡§®‡§Ç ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡•Ä‡§®‡•á ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡•á ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ã‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡•§ .. ‡§π‡•á ‡§â‡§∑‡§É ‡§Ø‡§§‡§É ‡§Ø‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§§‡•á‡§ú‡•ã‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø ‡§¶‡§¶‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á ‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•á ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ú‡§æ‡§∞‡§á‡§µ ‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§µ ‡§Ü‡§ö‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§™‡•á ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ö‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡•Ä‡§µ ‡§ú‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡•ç‡§ú‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§∞‡§ø ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ö‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡•á ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç ‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§£‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§µ ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ö‡§∞‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§Æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§§‡•Ä ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É‡•§ ‡§® ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•Ä‡§µ ‡§Ø‡§§‡•Ä ‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ö‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä‡§µ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•à‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡•§ ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•à‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á‡§ú‡•ã‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•á ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§É ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúMany are the days that have dawned before the rising of the sun, on which you, U·π£as, have beenbeheld like a wife going to an inconstant husband, and not like one deserting him.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Like a wife: yathƒÅ pari jƒÅraivƒÅcaranti… na punar yatƒ´vƒÅ: as in the world a virtuous woman is not seen abandoning a bad and vagranthusband, nor herself going astray

Jamison Brereton

Those were the days: the many days through which, at the rising of the sun,
you became visible, o Dawn‚Äîfaring forth thence toward the east-facing (sacrifice), like (a maiden) faring forth to her lover, not like one going (home) again.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Despite the straightforward, indeed ballad-like opening (‚Äúthose were the days ‚Ä¶‚Äù), the syntax of the rest of this vs. is difficult to entangle. The problem is that there appear to be two subordinating expressions (yƒÅÃÅin b, y√°ta·∏• p√°ri in c), though it is difficult to identify more than one subordinate clause; if there are two subordinate clauses, one of them would have very sketchy clausal structure. Nonetheless, Geldner and Renou opt for the latter solution, supplying a verb in b, both taking yƒÅÃÅas neut. pl. nom. and the subject of this clause (e.g., ‚ÄúNombreux furent ces jours en v√©rit√© qui (surgirent) autrefois ‚Ä¶‚Äù); for them cd is then a new subordinate cl. marked by y√°ta·∏• p√°ri referring to these same days (e.g., ‚Äú√† la suite desquels ‚Ä¶‚Äù). Something like this 101 is possible, and in my many fiddlings with this vs. over the years I have more than once hovered over something like it. But the stumbling block is prƒÅcƒ´ÃÅnam in b, which both Geldner and Renou must take as an adverbial temporal expression (‚Äúvorher‚Äù and ‚Äúautrefois‚Äù respectively), even though this stem is otherwise only locational ‚Äòforwards / towards the east‚Äô, often in a ritual context. I can see no way to integrate the standard use of this stem into a nominal clause consisting only of pƒÅda b. I therefore take bcd as a single subordinate clause with two markers of subordination, yƒÅÃÅ(b) a neut. pl. acc. extent of time (‚Äúthrough which ‚Ä¶) and y√°ta·∏• p√°ri (c) referring to the place from which Dawn comes, picking up pur√°stƒÅt in 2c. The yƒÅÃÅis more narrowly construed with the finite verb dad·πõk·π£√© in d (‚Äú‚Ä¶ the days through which you became visible‚Äù – that is, dawned over and over), the y√°ta·∏• p√°ri with the participle ƒÅc√°rantƒ´ ‚Äúfaring forth thence [= from the east].‚Äù I further take prƒÅcƒ´ÃÅnam as the goal of that participle (‚Äúfaring forth ‚Ä¶ towards the east-facing [sacrifice]‚Äù). As I just noted, prƒÅcƒ´ÃÅna- is often found in a ritual context, modifying yaj√±√°- (VII.7.3) or barh√≠s- (I.188.4, IV.5.4, X.110.4). Either would be possible here, and the point would be that Dawn is hastening from the east towards the sacrifice that, like an expectant lover, is facing towards her. Assuming with most comm., beginning with SƒÅya·πáa. (see esp. Oldenberg‚Äôs argumentation) that we should read loc. jƒÅr√©, contra Pp. jƒÅr√°·∏•, the acc. prƒÅcƒ´ÃÅnam in the frame would be the functional equivalent of jƒÅr√© in the simile – GOAL – despite the mismatch of cases, a nice example of case disharmony in a simile (as discussed in my 1982 IIJ article).

Although I realize that this is a very fussy solution, I cannot see any other way to deal with the troublesome prƒÅcƒ´ÃÅnam. And it is, after all, an omphalos vs., where perturbations are common. Strictly speaking, my tr. fails to render both subordinators as such: ‚Äúthence‚Äù should be ‚Äúwhence.‚Äù But the tr. is hard enough to parse as it is.

The contrastively paired similes, ‚Äúlike (a maiden) faring forth to her lover, not like one going (home) again‚Äù (jƒÅr√° ivƒÅÃÅc√°rantƒ´ ‚Ä¶ n√° p√∫nar yatƒ´ÃÅva), are well understood by the standard comm. and nicely indicate that Dawn dawns with as much speed as she can muster, eager for reunion with her lover, rather than lingering like one reluctantly leaving a tryst. For the first cf. I.123.9 ‚Ä¶ y√≥·π£ƒÅ n√° … ni·π£krÃ•t√°m ƒÅc√°rantƒ´ ‚Äúgoing to the appointed place like a maiden to a rendezvous,‚Äù also of Dawn (see also VI.75.4 in the weapon hymn). The iva is wrongly placed in the 2nd simile, but the poet had too many elements to fit in as it was.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these two vss. are defined as an omphalos, and this relationship is signaled by the patterned repetition of their first pƒÅdas: 3a tƒÅÃÅnƒ´ÃÅd ‚Ä¶ ƒÅsan / 4a t√° √≠d ‚Ä¶ ƒÅsan ‚Äújust those were ‚Ä¶‚Äù

Griffith

Great is, in truth, the number of the Mornings which were aforetime at the Sun’s uprising.
Since thou, O Dawn, hast been beheld repairing as to thy love, as one no more to leave him.

Geldner

Das waren zahlreiche Tage, die vorher bei Sonnenaufgang anbrachen, seitdem du, Usas, dich gezeigt hast wie eine Frau, die zu ihrem Buhlen kommt, nicht wie eine, die wieder geht.

Grassmann

Es waren zahlreich diese Tageshellen im Ost erscheinend bei der Sonne Aufgang, Von wo du kamst wie zum Geliebten eilend, o Morgenroth, als gingest du nicht wieder.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§á‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡§ø) ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§µ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø (‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡§ø) ‡§®‡•à‡§ï‡§ß‡§æ ‡§§‡•á‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§ø (‡§Ü‡§∏‡§®‡•ç) ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§™‡§•‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø (‡§Ø‡§æ) ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø (‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§É‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡•Ä‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡•Ä‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç (‡§â‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§æ) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø (‡§Ø‡§§‡§É) ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç (‡§™‡§∞‡§ø ‡§ú‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§á‡§µ) ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∏‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡§æ‡§®‡§ø (‡§â‡§∑‡§É) ‡§§‡•á‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§ø (‡§Ü‡§ö‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø (‡§¶‡§¶‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á) ‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á (‡§Ø‡§§‡•Ä‡§µ) ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ ‡§á‡§µ (‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§®) ‡§® ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡••‡•©‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúThose ancient sages, our ancestors, observant of truth, rejoicing together with the gods, discovered thehidden ligh, and, reciters of sincere prayers, they genitive rated the Dawn.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Those were the feasting companions of the gods: the sage poets of old, provided with truth.
The Fathers discovered the hidden light. With their mantras that come true, they generated the Dawn.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Although this vs. begins in the same way as vs. 3, the syntax is quite straightforward, with no dependent clauses and the Fathers as subject throughout.

Once again we might consider this an example of poetic repair, given the syntactic difficulties the previous vs. posed.

Griffith

They were the Gods’ companions at the banquet, the ancient sages true to Law Eternal.
The Fathers found the light that lay in darkness, and with effectual words begat the Morning.

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§π‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ! ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä, ‡§µ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§µ‡§ø, ‡§µ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡•Ä‡§® ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ó‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§ø‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§ï‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ ‡§â‡§®‡•ç‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§∏‡§´‡§≤ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§® ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§§‡§ï ‡§™‡§π‡•Å‡§Å‡§ö‡§æ‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡••‡•™‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúWhen the common herd (of cattle had been stolen) then, associating, they concurred, nor mutuallycontended; they obstructed not the sacrifices of the gods, but, unoffending, proceeded with the light (they hadrecovered).‚Äù

Lit. = in the common vast assembled they agree, nor do they strive mutually; they injure not theobservances of the gods, not harming, going with treasures, or with the vasus;

Vasubhi·∏• = u·π£asam tejobhi·∏•

Jamison Brereton

Come together in a common pen, they [=priests] act in unison; they do not marshal themselves in opposition.
They do not transgress the commandments of the gods, they who are not negligent and are united with the good ones.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted above, this vs. marks the transition to present-tense verbs and imperatives from the distant past of vss. 3-4. Who the subject of these verbs is in vs. 5 is not entirely clear. Until the very last syllable of the first hemistich, it is impossible to know even the gender, but the oddly positioned t√© at the end of pƒÅda b identifies the subject as masc.; up until then, since s√°·πÉgatƒÅsa·∏• could be either masc. 102 or fem., the fem. Dawns are a possibility. The second hemistich repeats the t√© immediately (c), and adds an unambig. masc. adj. √°mardhanta·∏• (as well as potentially ambig. yƒÅÃÅdamƒÅnƒÅ·∏•). Once t√© restricts the subject to masc., our immediate thought would be the Fathers, who are the subject of vs. 4. This is the solution of both Geldner and Renou However, the temporal switch between 4 and 5 might speak against that. In vs. 6 the Vasi·π£·π≠has are explicitly identified as the subject (6a). My own view is that the subject of vs. 5 is deliberately left unspecified, to allow a transition between, and identification of, the Fathers and their latter-day representatives the Vasi·π£·π≠has. That the Fathers are at least arguably present is suggested by samƒÅn√° ≈´rv√© ‚Äúin a common pen,‚Äù since ≈´rv√°- frequently refers to the Vala cave where the cows/dawns are confined and therefore could set the action of the vs. in mythological time when, as the preceding vs. notes, the Fathers ‚Äúfound the hidden light‚Äù and ‚Äúgenerated the dawns,‚Äù as in the Vala myth. As for a contemporary reference, ‚Äúcommon pen‚Äù could refer to the sacrificial ground, where the Vasi·π£·π≠has would be acting in concert.

In addition to specifying the gender of the subject of ab, the final t√© also repeats the final syllables of the two verbs that precede it in the pƒÅda, jƒÅnate ‚Ä¶ yatante.

Griffith

Meeting together in the same enclosure, they strive not, ofone mind, one with another.
They never break the Gods’ eternal statutes, and injure none, in rivalry with Vasus.

Geldner

Um die gemeinsame eingesperrte Herde versammelt vertragen sie sich, nicht eifern sie gegeneinander. Nicht √ºbertreten sie die Gebote der G√∂tter, nicht zur√ºckstehend, mit den Vasu¬¥s zusammengehend.

Grassmann

Zu gleichem Orte allesammt gekommen sind eines Sinns sie, liegen nicht im Streite, Verletzen nicht die g√∂ttlichen Gesetze, die rastlos gehn vereinigt mit den guten.

English translation:

‚ÄúAuspicious U·π£as, the Vasi·π£·π≠has, waking at dawn; and praising you, glorify you, glorify you with hymns;U·π£as, who are the conductress of the cattle (to pasture), the bestower of food, dawn upon us; shine, well-bornU·π£as, the first (of the gods).‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

In response to you the Vasi·π£·π≠has reverently invoke you with praises, when, waking at dawn, they have praised you, well-portioned one.
Leader of cows, mistress of prizes, dawn for us, o well-born Dawn. Be first awake.

Griffith

Extolling thee, Blest Goddess, the Vasisthas, awake at early mom, with lauds implore thee.
Leader of kine and Queen of all that strengthens, shine, come as first to us, O high-born Morning.

Geldner

Dich rufen wieder die Vasistha¬¥s mit Preisliedern, in der Fr√ºhe erwacht, du Holde, unter Lobges√§ngen. F√ºhrerin der K√ºhe, Herrin der Belohnungen, leuchte du uns auf; erwache als die Erste, du edelgeborene Usas!

Grassmann

Mit Lobges√§ngen preisen die Vasischtha’s, dich selige besingend fr√ºh am Morgen, Der Labung Herrin, Rinder f√ºhrend, leuchte, als erste nahe uns, o edle Uschas.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§è‡§∑‡§æ ‡§â‡§∑‡§æ‡§É) ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§â‡§∑‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤ (‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§∏‡§É ‡§®‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä) ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§∏‡•Ç‡§®‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç) ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ (‡§â‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ (‡§µ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡•à‡§É ‡§∞‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•ã‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§π‡•à, ‡§á‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç (‡§¶‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§ò‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§Ç) ‡§ö‡§ø‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡•Ä‡§® ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§π‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç (‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§æ) ‡§ß‡§® ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§®‡§É) ‡§π‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§ß‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§Ç) ‡§Ü‡§™ (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á (‡§∏‡§¶‡§æ) ‡§∏‡§¶‡§æ (‡§™‡§æ‡§§) ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç‡••‡•≠‡••

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§π‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ! ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§â‡§∑‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§Ü‡§™ ‡§π‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§π‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§Ø‡§ú‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§™‡§ï‡§æ ‡§¶‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§π‡•ã ‡•• ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§â‡§∑‡§æ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§®‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ó‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§µ‡§π ‡§â‡§™‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡•à, ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§â‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ê‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§§ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§â‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•Ä ‡§¨‡§®‡§æ ‡§¶‡•á‡§§‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§á‡§∏‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ó‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§µ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§∑‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§ú‡•ú ‡§π‡•ã‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ï‡§ø‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§ï ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Ä ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç, ‡§∏‡§¨‡§ï‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Ä ‡§è‡§ï‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§â‡§∏‡§∏‡•á ‡§≠‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã‡§à ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡••‡•≠‡•• ‡§Ø‡§π ‡•≠‡•¨‡§µ‡§æ‡§Å ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡•®‡•©‡§µ‡§æ‡§Å ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü ‡••

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø (‡§∏‡§Ç) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§•‡•ã‡§∑‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§®‡•à‡§É ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡•§

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø (‡§∏‡§Ç)- ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§è‡§∑‡§æ ‡§â‡§∑‡§æ‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§∑‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡§É (‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§∏‡§É ‡§®‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä) ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§•‡§æ‡§Ç (‡§∏‡•Ç‡§®‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§µ‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§≠‡§ø‡§É (‡§â‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§æ (‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡•à‡§É ‡§∞‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ó‡•Å‡§£-‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§µ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø, ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡•ã‡§∑‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡•á (‡§¶‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§ò‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ö‡§ø‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§É ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§®‡§É (‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§¶‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§æ) ‡§ß‡§®‡§Ç ‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§Ø‡§§‡•Å, ‡§§‡§•‡§æ (‡§®‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§ß‡§®‡§Ç (‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§®‡•á‡§® (‡§∏‡§¶‡§æ) ‡§∂‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç (‡§™‡§æ‡§§) ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡••‡•≠‡•• ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§∑‡§ü‡•ç‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡§ø‡§§‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§µ‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∂‡•ã ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§É ‡••
